</p><p>
<br>
</p><h2>FOOTNOTES
</h2>
<br>
<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn5.1" href="#fr5.1">1
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
<p>
</p><p>&nbsp;In other words, a facial attack, since it requires unconstitutionality in all circumstances, necessarily presumes that the litigant presently before the court would be able to sustain an as-applied challenge.  See 
<em>Hoffman Estates
</em> v. 
<em>Flipside, Hoffman Estates, Inc.,
</em> 
455 U.&nbsp;S. 489, 495
 (1982) ("A plaintiff who engages in some conduct that is clearly proscribed cannot complain of the vagueness of the law as applied to
<br>the conduct of others.  A court should therefore examine the complainant's conduct before analyzing other hypothetical applications of the law"); 
<em>Parker 
</em>v. 
<em>Levy
</em>, 
417 U.&nbsp;S. 733, 756
 (1974) ("One to whose con-
<br>duct a statute clearly applies may not successfully challenge it for vagueness").  
</p><p>
</p><p>The plurality asserts that in 
<em>United States 
</em>v. 
<em>Salerno
</em>, 
481 U.&nbsp;S. 739
 (1987), which I discuss in text immediately following this footnote, the Court "entertained" a facial challenge even though "the defendants . . . did not claim that the statute was unconstitutional as applied to them."  
<em>Ante
</em>, at 11, n. 22.  That is not so.  The Court made it absolutely clear in 
<em>Salerno
</em> that a facial challenge requires the assertion that "
<em>no set of circumstances exists
</em> under which the Act would be valid," 
481 U.&nbsp;S., at 745
 (emphasis added).  The footnoted statement upon which the plurality relies ("Nor have respondents claimed that the Act is unconstitutional because of the way it was applied to the particular facts of their case," 
<em>id
</em>., at 745, n.&nbsp;3) was obviously meant to convey the fact that the defendants were not making, 
<em>in addition to their facial challenge
</em>, an alternative as-applied challenge--
<em>i.e.
</em>, asserting that 
<em>even if
</em> the statute was not unconstitutional in 
<em>all
</em> its applications it was 
<em>at least
</em> unconstitutional in its particular application to them.
</p><p>
<br>
<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn5.2" href="#fr5.2">2
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
</p><p>
</p><p>&nbsp;
<em>Salerno,
</em> a criminal case, repudiated the Court's statement in 
<em>Kolender 
</em>v. 
<em>Lawson
</em>, 
461 U.&nbsp;S. 352, 359
, n.&nbsp;8 (1983), to the effect that a facial challenge to a criminal statute could succeed "even when [the statute] could conceivably have had some valid application."  
<em>Kolender
</em> seems to have confused the standard for First Amendment overbreadth challenges with the standard governing facial challenges on all other grounds.  See 
<em>ibid.
</em> (citing the Court's articulation of the standard for 
<em>First Amendment overbreadth 
</em>challenges from 
<em>Hoffman Estates, supra,
</em> at 494).  As 
<em>Salerno
</em> noted, 
481 U.&nbsp;S., at 745
, the overbreadth doctrine is a specialized exception to the general rule for facial challenges, justified in light of the risk that an overbroad statute will chill free expression.  See, 
<em>e.g.,
</em> 
<em>Broadrick
</em> v. 
<em>Oklahoma
</em>, 
413 U. S. 601, 612
 (1973).
</p><p>
<br>
<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn5.3" href="#fr5.3">3
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
</p><p>
</p><p> The plurality asserts that the 
<em>Salerno 
</em>standard for facial challenge "has never been the decisive factor in any decision of this Court."  It means by that only this: in rejecting a facial challenge, the Court has never contented itself with identifying 
<em>only one
</em> situation in which the challenged statute would be constitutional, but has mentioned several.  But that is not at all remarkable, and casts no doubt upon the validity of the principle that 
<em>Salerno
</em> and these many other cases enunciated.  It is difficult to conceive of a statute that would be constitutional in only a single application--and hard to resist mentioning more than one.
</p><p>
</p><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The plurality contends that it 
<em>does not matter
</em> whether the 
<em>Salerno
</em> standard is federal law, since facial challenge is a species of third-party standing, and federal limitations upon third-party standing do not apply in an appeal from a state decision which takes a broader view, as the Illinois Supreme Court's opinion did here.  
<em>Ante
</em>, at 11, n. 22.  This is quite wrong.  Disagreement over the 
<em>Salerno
</em> rule is not a disagreement over the "standing" question of whether the person challenging the statute can 
<em>raise
</em> the rights of third parties: under both 
<em>Salerno
</em> and the plurality's rule he 
<em>can
</em>.  The disagreement relates to 
<em>how many
</em> third-party rights he must 
<em>prove to be infringed
</em> by the statute before he can 
<em>win
</em>: 
<em>Salerno
</em> says "all" (in addition to his own rights), the plurality says "many."  That is not a question of standing but of substantive law.  The notion that, if 
<em>Salerno
</em> is the federal rule (a federal statute is not totally invalid unless it is invalid in all its applications), it can be 
<em>altered
</em> by a state court (a federal statute is totally invalid if it is invalid in 
<em>many
</em> of its applications), and that that alteration must be accepted by the Supreme Court of the United States is, to put it as gently as possible, remarkable.
</p><p>
<br>
<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn5.4" href="#fr5.4">4
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
</p><p>
</p><p>&nbsp;See, 
<em>e.g.,
</em> 
<em>Abdullah 
</em>v. 
<em>Commissioner of Ins. of Commonwealth of Mass.
</em>, 84 F.&nbsp;3d 18, 20 (CA1 1996); 
<em>Deshawn E. 
</em>v. 
<em>Safir
</em>, 156 F.&nbsp;3d 340, 347 (CA2 1998); 
<em>Artway 
</em>v. 
<em>Attorney Gen. of State of N.&nbsp;J.
</em>, 81 F.&nbsp;3d 1235, 1252, n.&nbsp;13 (CA3 1996); 
<em>Manning 
</em>v. 
<em>Hunt
</em>, 119 F.&nbsp;3d 254, 268-269 (CA4 1997); 
<em>Causeway Medical Suite 
</em>v. 
<em>Ieyoub
</em>, 109 F.&nbsp;3d 1096, 1104 (CA5), cert. denied, 
522 U.&nbsp;S. 943
 (1997); 
<em>Aronson 
</em>v. 
<em>City of Akron
</em>, 116 F.&nbsp;3d 804, 809 (CA6 1997); 
<em>Government Suppliers Consolidating Servs., Inc.
</em> v. 
<em>Bayh
</em>, 975 F.&nbsp;2d 1267, 1283 (CA7 1992), cert. denied, 
506 U.&nbsp;S. 1053
 (1993); 
<em>Woodis 
</em>v. 
<em>Westark Community College
</em>, 160 F.&nbsp;3d 435, 438-439 (CA8 1998); 
<em>Roulette 
</em>v. 
<em>Seattle
</em>, 97 F.&nbsp;3d 300, 306 (CA9 1996); 
<em>Public Lands Council 
</em>v. 
<em>Babbitt
</em>, 167 F.&nbsp;3d 1287, 1293 (CA10 1999); 
<em>Dimmitt 
</em>v. 
<em>Clearwater
</em>, 985 F.&nbsp;2d 1565, 1570-1571 (CA11 1993); 
<em>Time Warner Entertainment Co. 
</em>v. 
<em>FCC
</em>, 93 F.&nbsp;3d 957, 972 (CADC 1996).
</p><p>
<br>
<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn5.5" href="#fr5.5">5
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
</p><p>
</p><p>&nbsp;With apologies for taking creative license with the work of Messrs. Bernstein, Sondheim, and Laurents.  West Side Story, copyright 1959.
</p><p>
<br>
<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn5.6" href="#fr5.6">6
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
</p><p>
</p><p>&nbsp;The plurality's explanation for ignoring these laws is that many of them carried severe penalties and, during the Reconstruction era, they had "harsh consequences on African-American women and children."  
<em>Ante
</em>, at 9-10, n.&nbsp;20.  Those severe penalties and those harsh consequences are certainly regrettable, but they in no way lessen (indeed, the harshness of penalty tends to increase) the capacity of these laws to 
<em>prove
</em> that loitering was never regarded as a fundamental liberty.
</p><p>
<br>
<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn5.7" href="#fr5.7">7
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
</p><p>
</p><p>&nbsp;
<em>Ante
</em>, at 9, n.&nbsp;19.  The plurality bases its assertion of apparent concession upon a footnote in Part I of petitioner's brief which reads: "Of course, laws regulating social gatherings affect a liberty interest, and thus are subject to review under the rubric of substantive due process . . . . We address that doctrine in Part II below."  Brief for Petitioner 21-22, n.&nbsp;14.  If a careless reader were inclined to confuse the term "social gatherings" in this passage with "loitering," his confusion would be eliminated by pursuing the reference to Part II of the brief, which says, in its introductory paragraph: "[A]s we explain below, substantive due process does not support the court's novel holding that the Constitution secures the right to stand still on the public way even when one is not engaged in speech, assembly, or other conduct that enjoys affirmative constitutional protection."  
<em>Id
</em>., at 39.
</p><p>
<br>
<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn5.8" href="#fr5.8">8
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
</p><p>
</p><p>&nbsp;The plurality says, 
<em>ante
</em>, at 20, n. 35, that since it decides the case on the basis of 
<em>procedural
</em> due process rather than 
<em>substantive 
</em>due process, I am mistaken in analyzing its opinion "under the framework for substantive due process set out in 
<em>Washington
</em> v. 
<em>Glucksberg.
</em>"
<em>  Ibid
</em>.  But I am not analyzing it under that framework.  I am simply assuming that when the plurality says (as an essential part of its reasoning) that "the right to loiter for innocent purposes is . . . a part of the liberty protected by the Due Process Clause" it does not believe that the same word ("liberty") means one thing for purposes of substantive due process and something else for purposes of procedural due process.  There is no authority for that startling proposition.  See 
<em>Board of Regents of State Colleges
</em> v. 
<em>Roth
</em>, 
408 U.&nbsp;S. 564, 572-575
 (1972) (rejecting procedural-due-process claim for lack of "liberty" interest, and citing substantive-due-process cases).
</p><p>
</p><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The plurality's opinion seeks to have it both ways, invoking the Fourteenth Amendment's august protection of "liberty" in defining the standard of certainty that it sets, but then, in identifying the conduct protected by that high standard, ignoring our extensive case-law defining "liberty," and substituting, instead, all "harmless and innocent" conduct, 
<em>ante
</em>, at 14.
</p><p>
<br>
<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn5.9" href="#fr5.9">9
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
</p><p>
</p><p>&nbsp;I call it a "suggestion" because the plurality says only that the terms of the dispersal order "compound the inadequacy of the notice," and acknowledges that they "might not render the ordinance unconstitutionally vague if the definition of the forbidden conduct were clear."  
<em>Ante
</em>, at 15, 16.  This notion that a prescription ("Disperse!") which is itself not unconstitutionally vague can somehow contribute to the unconstitutional vagueness of the entire scheme is full of mystery--suspending, as it does, the metaphysical principle that nothing can confer what it does not possess (
<em>nemo dat qui non habet
</em>). 
</p><p>
<br>
<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn5.10" href="#fr5.10">10
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
</p><p>
</p><p>&nbsp;"Administrative interpretation and implementation of a regulation are ... highly relevant to our [vagueness] analysis, for `[i]n evaluating a facial challenge to a state law, a federal court must ... consider any limiting construction that a state court 
<em>or enforcement agency
</em> has proffered.'&nbsp;"  
<em>Ward 
</em>v. 
<em>Rock Against Racism
</em>, 
491 U.&nbsp;S. 781, 795-796
 (1989)  (emphasis added) (quoting 
<em>Hoffman Estates, 
</em>
455 U.&nbsp;S., at 494
, n.&nbsp;5).  See also 
<em>Hoffman Estates
</em>, 455 U.&nbsp;S.,
<em> 
</em>at 504 (administrative regulations "will often suffice to clarify a standard with an otherwise uncertain scope").
</p><p>
<br>
<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn5.11" href="#fr5.11">11
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
</p><p>
</p><p>
<em>&nbsp;Justice Breyer
</em> asserts that "one always has some apparent purpose," so that the policeman must "interpret the words `no apparent purpose' as meaning `no apparent purpose except for . . . .'"  
<em>Ante
</em>, at 1-2.  It is simply not true that "one always has some apparent purpose"--and especially not true that one always has some apparent purpose in remaining at rest, for the simple reason that one often (indeed, perhaps usually) has no 
<em>actual
</em> purpose in remaining at rest.  Remaining at rest will be a person's normal state, unless he has a purpose which causes him to move.  That is why one frequently reads of a person's "wandering aimlessly" (which is worthy of note) but not of a person's "sitting aimlessly" (which is not remarkable at all).  And that is why a synonym for "purpose" is "motive": that which causes one 
<em>to move
</em>.
</p><p>
<br>
<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn5.12" href="#fr5.12">12
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
</p><p>
</p><p>&nbsp;The Court also speculates that a police officer may exercise his discretion to 
<em>enforce
</em> the Ordinance and 
<em>direct
</em> dispersal when (in the Court's view) the Ordinance is inapplicable--viz., where there 
<em>is
</em> an apparent purpose, but it is an unlawful one.  See 
<em>ante
</em>, at 18.  No one in his right mind would read the phrase "without any apparent purpose" to mean anything other than "without any apparent lawful purpose."  The implication that acts referred to approvingly in statutory language are "lawful" acts is routine.  The Court asserts that the Illinois Supreme Court has forced it into this interpretive inanity because, since it "has not placed any limiting construction on the language in the ordinance, we must assume that the ordinance means what it says . . . ."  
<em>Ante
</em>, at 19.  But the Illinois Supreme Court did not mention this particular interpretive issue, which has nothing to do with giving the Ordinance a "limiting" interpretation, and everything to do with giving it its ordinary legal meaning.
</p><p>
<br>
<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn5.13" href="#fr5.13">13
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
</p><p>
</p><p>&nbsp;The opinion that 
<em>Justice Breyer 
</em>relies on, 
<em>Coates
</em> v. 
<em>Cincinnati
</em>, 
402 U.&nbsp;S. 611
 (1971), discussed 
<em>ante
</em>, at 3-4, did not say that the ordinance there at issue gave adequate notice but did not provide adequate standards for the police.  It invalidated that ordinance 
<em>on both inadequate-notice and inadequate-enforcement-standard grounds
</em>, because First Amendment rights were implicated.  It is common ground, however, that the present case does not implicate the First Amendment, see 
<em>ante
</em>, at 8-9 (plurality opinion); 
<em>ante
</em>, at 3 (
<em>Breyer, J.,
</em> concurring in part and concurring in judgment).
</p><p>

<br>
<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn6.5" href="#fr6.5">5
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
</p><p>
</p><p>&nbsp;The other cases upon which the plurality relies concern the entirely distinct right to interstate and international travel.  See 
<em>Williams
</em> v. 
<em>Fears,
</em> 
179 U.&nbsp;S. 270, 274-275
 (1900); 
<em>Kent
</em> v. 
<em>Dulles,
</em> 
357 U.&nbsp;S. 116
 (1958).  The plurality claims that dicta in those cases articulating a right of free movement, see 
<em>Williams, supra,
</em> at 274; 
<em>Kent, supra,
</em> at 125, also supports an individual's right to "remain in a public place of his choice."  Ironically, 
<em>Williams
</em> rejected the argument that a tax on persons engaged in the business of importing out-of-state labor impeded the freedom of transit, so the precise holding in that case does not support, but undermines, the plurality's view.  Similarly, the precise holding in 
<em>Kent
</em> did not bear on a constitutional right to travel; instead, the Court held only that Congress had not authorized the Secretary of State to deny certain passports.  Furthermore, the plurality's approach distorts the principle articulated in those cases, stretching it to a level of generality that permits the Court to disregard the relevant historical evidence that should guide the analysis.  
<em>Michael&nbsp;H.
</em> v. 
<em>Gerald&nbsp;D.,
</em> 
491 U.&nbsp;S. 110, 127
, n.&nbsp;6 (1989) (plurality opinion).
</p><p>

<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn6.9" href="#fr6.9">9
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
</p><p>
</p><p>&nbsp;The plurality suggests, 
<em>ante
</em>, at 15, that dispersal orders are, by their nature, vague. The plurality purports to distinguish its sweeping condemnation of dispersal orders from 
<em>Colten 
</em>v. 
<em>Kentucky
</em>, 
407 U.&nbsp;S. 104
 (1972), but I see no principled ground for doing so.  The logical implication of the plurality's assertion is that the police can never issue dispersal orders.  For example, in the plurality's view, it is apparently unconstitutional for a police officer to ask a group of gawkers to move along in order to secure a crime scene.
</p><p>
<br>
<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn6.10" href="#fr6.10">10
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
</p><p>
</p><p>&nbsp;For example, a 1764 Georgia law declared that "all able bodied persons ... who shall be found loitering ... all other idle vagrants, or disorderly persons wandering abroad without betaking themselves to some lawful employer or honest labor, shall be deemed and adjudged vagabonds," and required the apprehension of "any such vagabond&nbsp;... found within any county in this State, wandering, strolling, loitering about." (reprinted in The First Laws of the State of Georgia, Part&nbsp;1, 376-377 (J.&nbsp;Cushing comp. 1981)).  See also, 
<em>e.g.
</em>, Digest of the Laws of Pennsylvania 829 (F.&nbsp;Brightly ed., 8th ed. 1853) ("The following described persons shall be liable to the penalties imposed by law upon vagrants&nbsp;...&nbsp;.  All persons who shall ... be found loitering"); Ky. Rev. Stat., ch.&nbsp;CIV, 1, p.&nbsp;69 (1852) ("If any able bodied person be found loitering or rambling about,&nbsp;... he shall be taken and adjudged to be a vagrant, and guilty of a high misdemeanor").
</p><p>
<br>
<strong>Footnote 
<a name="fn6.11" href="#fr6.11">11
</a>&nbsp;
</strong>
<br>
</p><p>
</p><p>&nbsp;The Court asserts that we cannot second-guess the Illinois Supreme Court's conclusion that the definition "&nbsp;`provides absolute discretion to police officers to determine what activities constitute loitering,'&nbsp;" 
<em>ante,
</em> at 17 (quoting 117 Ill. 2d 440, 457, 687 N.&nbsp;E. 2d 53, 63 (1997).  While we are bound by a state court's construction of a statute, the Illinois court "did not, strictly speaking, construe the [ordinance] in the sense of defining the meaning of a particular statutory word or phase.  Rather, it merely characterized [its] `practical effect'&nbsp;...&nbsp;.  This assessment does not bind us."  
<em>Wisconsin 
</em>v. 
<em>Mitchell
</em>, 
508 U.&nbsp;S. 476, 484
 (1993).
</p><p>

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {

  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());

    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

  }

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}

  try {

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      let toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if (practiceCode) {
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode;
      }
      _satellite.logger.info('s_dsource',toStore);
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
        });
    }


    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    const searchComponents = document.querySelectorAll('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    _satellite.logger.info('searchComponents',searchComponents);
    searchComponents.forEach((component) => {
      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })
    

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - click
    const submitButtons = document.querySelectorAll('submit-button, .hero-content input[type="submit"], .search-secondary input[type="submit"]')
    submitButtons.forEach((button) => {
      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search
    if (location.host + location.pathname === "www.findlaw.com/") {
      const forms = document.querySelectorAll("form[action*='stdSearch_process.jsp']");
      _satellite.logger.info('forms',forms);
      forms.forEach((form) => {
        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);
        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

    //CTA
    const links = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="fli="], .articleHeading p a[href*="lawyers.findlaw.com"]');
    _satellite.logger.info('links',links);
    links.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        const goTo = link.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        let source = link.dataset.searchLocation || '';
        if (!source) {
          if (link.closest('.cta-public')) {
            source = 'CTA: Banner';
          } else {
            source = 'CTA: Inline Link';
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });
    });

    //Right Rail
    const sidebarLawyerSearch = document.querySelector('.sidebarLawyerSearch');
    _satellite.logger.info('sidebarLawyerSearch',sidebarLawyerSearch);
    if (sidebarLawyerSearch) {
          sidebarLawyerSearch.addEventListener('click', function(event) {
            if (event.target.getAttribute('href') && event.target.getAttribute('href').includes('lawyers.findlaw.com')) {
              const goTo = event.target.getAttribute('href');
              _satellite.slowDown(event, goTo);
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Link Module: Right Rail");
            }
          });
    }

    //Browse
    if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
      const lawyerLinks = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="/lawyer"]');
      _satellite.logger.info('lawyerLinks',lawyerLinks);
      lawyersLinks.forEach((link) => {
        link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
          const goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
          _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
          let source = "";
          if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statepractice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Popular Cities";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/state/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: State";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statecounty/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Top Counties";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/practice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Legal Issue";
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        });
      });

        _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
        _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
      });
    });
